## Evidence Inventory

### Claim 1: FastAPI already ships a canonical timing middleware pattern.

**VERIFIED** — `fastapi/applications.py:4628-4636` contains the `@app.middleware("http")` decorator with an `add_process_time_header` example in its docstring. The same pattern lives in `docs_src/middleware/tutorial001_py310.py:8-13` (uses `time.perf_counter()` and sets `X-Process-Time` header). The associated test is at `tests/test_tutorial/test_middleware/test_tutorial001.py:9-12`.

```python
# fastapi/applications.py:4628-4636 — docstring example
@app.middleware("http")
async def add_process_time_header(
    request: Request, call_next: Callable[[Request], Awaitable[Response]]
) -> Response:
    start_time = time.time()
    response = await call_next(request)
    process_time = time.time() - start_time
    response.headers["X-Process-Time"] = str(process_time)
    return response
```

### Claim 2: The decorator wraps Starlette `BaseHTTPMiddleware`.

**VERIFIED** — `fastapi/applications.py:4641`:
```python
self.add_middleware(BaseHTTPMiddleware, dispatch=func)
```
This adds to `self.user_middleware` (`applications.py:1012-1013`).

### Claim 3: User middleware is placed between `ServerErrorMiddleware` and `ExceptionMiddleware` in the stack.

**VERIFIED** — `fastapi/applications.py:1031-1060` (`build_middleware_stack`):
```
ServerErrorMiddleware
+ self.user_middleware          ← timing middleware goes here
+ ExceptionMiddleware
+ AsyncExitStackMiddleware
+ self.router
```
The stack at `applications.py:1064-1065` is built with `reversed(middleware)`.

### Claim 4: No FastAPI source code changes are needed — this is pure user-space configuration.

**VERIFIED** — The entire timing middleware can be implemented using only the public `@app.middleware("http")` API. No modification to any file under `fastapi/` is required.

### Claim 5: A custom ASGI middleware class (lower-level alternative) could be written, but is unnecessary.

**HYPOTHESIS** — The pattern at `fastapi/middleware/asyncexitstack.py:8-18` shows the ASGI-class style (`__init__(self, app, ...)` + `async __call__(self, scope, receive, send)`) used for framework-internal middleware. This is the right pattern if you need access to raw ASGI events (e.g., `receive`/`send` to measure body read time separately). For simple total request timing, `@app.middleware("http")` is sufficient.

### Claim 6: A `Server-Timing` header variant would require different formatting.

**HYPOTHESIS** — The twin results have `COV.MEASURE`, `COV.INTAKE`, `COV.ROUTE` — suggesting route-level total timing. A richer `Server-Timing` header (e.g., `Server-Timing: total;dur=12.3`) is not yet established in this codebase (no grep hits). The safest path sticks to the existing `X-Process-Time` / `X-Request-Timing` convention.

### Claim 7: Twin files are test files, not production code — use them as test-pattern reference.

**VERIFIED** — All three top twins are in `tests/`. The seam cluster `cluster_tests_test_sub_callbacks_py_GET:` links to `cluster_tests_test_default_response_clas` (PREDICTED edge, `COV.FETCH→COV.PERSIST`). This suggests the new code should follow existing test patterns for middleware (e.g., `tests/test_tutorial/test_middleware/test_tutorial001.py`).

---

## Safest Implementation Path

### Strategy: **User-space `@app.middleware("http")` — zero framework modification.**

Changes touch exactly **one new file** (user application code) and **one test file**:

```
┌─ Create ───────────────────────────────┐
│  app.py (or existing app module)       │
│  ├─ import time, from fastapi import * │
│  ├─ @app.middleware("http")            │
│  │   async def add_request_timing(...) │
│  │   ... measure using perf_counter    │
│  └─ set response.headers[...]          │
├────────────────────────────────────────┤
│  tests/test_timing_middleware.py       │
│  └─ TestClient + assert header exists  │
└────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**No changes to:** `fastapi/applications.py`, `fastapi/routing.py`, `fastapi/middleware/*`, `starlette` — zero cross-boundary impact.

### Code to write (user-space, not framework):

```python
import time
from fastapi import FastAPI, Request

app = FastAPI()

@app.middleware("http")
async def add_request_timing_header(request: Request, call_next):
    start_time = time.perf_counter()
    response = await call_next(request)
    process_time = time.perf_counter() - start_time
    response.headers["X-Request-Timing"] = f"{process_time:.4f}"
    return response
```

### Test pattern (follows `tests/test_tutorial/test_middleware/test_tutorial001.py`):

```python
from fastapi.testclient import TestClient

from app import app

client = TestClient(app)

def test_request_timing_header():
    response = client.get("/openapi.json")
    assert response.status_code == 200
    assert "X-Request-Timing" in response.headers
```

| # | Action | Status | Reference |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | Confirm you want `X-Request-Timing` vs `X-Process-Time` vs `Server-Timing` | ⬜ decide | `docs_src/middleware/tutorial001_py310.py:13` uses `X-Process-Time` |
| 2 | Write the `@app.middleware("http")` function in your app module | ⬜ do | pattern: `fastapi/applications.py:4628-4636` |
| 3 | Use `time.perf_counter()` not `time.time()` for sub-second precision | ⬜ do | `docs_src/middleware/tutorial001_py310.py:10,12` |
| 4 | Write test using `TestClient(app)` asserting `"X-Request-Timing" in response.headers` | ⬜ do | pattern: `tests/test_tutorial/test_middleware/test_tutorial001.py:9-12` |
| 5 | Verify the middleware correctly handles exceptions (middleware is above `ExceptionMiddleware` in stack — `fastapi/applications.py:1034-1039` so exceptions inside routes will still propagate up to the middleware) | ⬜ verify | stack order: `applications.py:1031-1060` |
| 6 | If you need sub-route timing (DB query time, etc.), extend inside the route handler instead | ⬜ optional | beyond scope of current `COV.MEASURE`/`INTAKE`/`ROUTE` cov |
| 7 | Run `pytest tests/test_timing_middleware.py` (or your test file) | ⬜ final | — |
